<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_South_Africa&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>

<p><b>Apartheid</b> (<span class="nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/South_African_English" title="South African English">ZA</a> pronunciation:</small> <span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/ɑːr/ 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/eɪ/ long 'a' in 'base'">eɪ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <small>Afrikaans: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Afrikaans" title="Help:IPA for Afrikaans">[ɐˈpɐrtɦəit]</a></span>) was a system of institutionalised <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation" title="Racial segregation">racial segregation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">discrimination</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> between 1948 and 1991, when it was abolished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abolished_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abolished-1">[1]</a></sup> The country's first <a href="/wiki/South_African_general_election,_1994" title="South African general election, 1994">multiracial elections</a> under a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_franchise" title="Universal franchise">universal franchise</a> were held three years later in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell-2">[2]</a></sup> Broadly speaking, apartheid was delineated into <i>petty apartheid</i>, which entailed the segregation of public facilities and social events, and <i>grand apartheid</i>, which dictated housing and employment opportunities by race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crompton_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crompton-3">[3]</a></sup> Prior to the 1940s, some vestiges of apartheid had already emerged in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minority_rule" title="Minority rule">minority rule</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_South_Africans" title="White South Africans">white South Africans</a> and the socially enforced separation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_South_Africans" title="Black South Africans">black South Africans</a> from other races, which later extended to <a href="/wiki/Pass_laws" title="Pass laws">pass laws</a> and land apportionment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-War_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-War-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Breckenridge_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Breckenridge-5">[5]</a></sup> Apartheid as a policy was embraced by the South African government shortly after the ascension of the <a href="/wiki/National_Party_(South_Africa)" title="National Party (South Africa)">National Party</a> (NP) during the country's <a href="/wiki/South_African_general_election,_1948" title="South African general election, 1948">1948 general elections</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ottoway_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ottoway-6">[6]</a></sup> Apartheid was also enforced in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_West_Africa" title="South West Africa">South West Africa</a> until it gained independence as <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a> in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>A codified system of racial stratification began to take form in South Africa under the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Empire" title="Dutch Empire">Dutch Empire</a> in the late eighteenth century, although informal segregation was present much earlier due to social cleavages between Dutch colonists and a creolised, ethnically diverse slave population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glaser_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glaser-8">[8]</a></sup> With the rapid growth and industrialisation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Cape_Colony" title="British Cape Colony">British Cape Colony</a> in the nineteenth century, racial policies and laws became increasingly rigid. Cape legislation that discriminated specifically against black Africans began appearing shortly before 1900.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bickford-Smith_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bickford-Smith-9">[9]</a></sup> The policies of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boer_republic" title="Boer republic">Boer republics</a> were also racially exclusive; for instance, the constitution of the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Republic" title="South African Republic">Transvaal</a> barred nonwhite participation in church and state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dyzenhaus_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dyzenhaus-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first piece of apartheid legislation was the <a href="/wiki/Prohibition_of_Mixed_Marriages_Act,_1949" title="Prohibition of Mixed Marriages Act, 1949">Prohibition of Mixed Marriages Act, 1949</a>, which was followed closely by the <a href="/wiki/Immorality_Act" title="Immorality Act">Immorality Act</a> of 1950, making it illegal for most South African citizens to marry or pursue sexual relationships across racial lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Routledge_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-11">[11]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Population_Registration_Act,_1950" title="Population Registration Act, 1950">Population Registration Act, 1950</a> classified all South Africans into one of four racial groups based on appearance, known ancestry, socioeconomic status, and cultural lifestyle: "black", "white", "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coloured" title="Coloured">coloured</a>", and "Indian", the last two of which included several sub-classifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Places of residence were determined by racial classification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Routledge_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-11">[11]</a></sup> From 1960 to 1983, 3.5 million nonwhite South Africans were removed from their homes, and forced into segregated neighbourhoods, in one of the largest mass removals in modern history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forced_Removals_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forced_Removals-13">[13]</a></sup> Most of these targeted removals were intended to restrict the black population to ten designated "tribal homelands", also known as <i><a href="/wiki/Bantustan" title="Bantustan">bantustans</a></i>, four of which became nominally independent states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Routledge_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-11">[11]</a></sup> The government announced that relocated persons would lose their South African citizenship as they were absorbed into the bantustans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crompton_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Crompton-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Apartheid sparked significant international and domestic opposition, resulting in some of the most influential global <a href="/wiki/Social_movement" title="Social movement">social movements</a> of the twentieth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lodge_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lodge-14">[14]</a></sup> It was the target of frequent condemnation in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, and brought about an extensive <a href="/wiki/Arms_embargo" title="Arms embargo">arms</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_embargo" title="Trade embargo">trade embargo</a> on South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lodge_1983_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lodge_1983-15">[15]</a></sup> During the 1970s and 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Internal_resistance_to_apartheid" title="Internal resistance to apartheid">internal resistance to apartheid</a> became increasingly militant, prompting brutal crackdowns by the National Party administration and protracted sectarian violence that left thousands dead or in detention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pandey_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pandey-16">[16]</a></sup> Some reforms of the apartheid system were undertaken, including allowing for <a href="/wiki/Tricameral_Parliament" title="Tricameral Parliament">Indian and coloured political representation in parliament</a>, but these measures failed in appeasing most activist groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diplomacy_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Diplomacy-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between 1987 and 1993 the National Party entered into <a href="/wiki/Negotiations_to_end_apartheid_in_South_Africa" title="Negotiations to end apartheid in South Africa">bilateral negotiations</a> with the <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress" title="African National Congress">African National Congress</a>, the leading anti-apartheid political movement, for ending segregation and introducing majority rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diplomacy_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Diplomacy-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In 1990, prominent ANC leaders such as <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a> were released from detention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Apartheid legislation was abolished in mid-1991,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abolished_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abolished-1">[1]</a></sup> pending multiracial elections set for April 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Apartheid</i> is an <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">Afrikaans</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> word meaning "separateness", or "the state of being apart", literally "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/apart#English" title="wikt:apart">apart</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/-hood#English" title="wikt:-hood">-hood</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Its first recorded use was in 1929.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Routledge_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precursors">Precursors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Precursors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_South_Africa_(1815%E2%80%931910)" title="History of South Africa (1815–1910)">History of South Africa (1815–1910)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_South_Africa_(1910%E2%80%931948)" title="History of South Africa (1910–1948)">History of South Africa (1910–1948)</a></div>
<p>Under the 1806 Cape Articles of Capitulation the new <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> colonial rulers were required to respect previous legislation enacted under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Dutch_law" title="Roman Dutch law">Roman Dutch law</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Introduction_to_Roman-Dutch_Law_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Introduction_to_Roman-Dutch_Law-23">[23]</a></sup> and this led to a separation of the law in South Africa from <a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">English Common Law</a> and a high degree of legislative autonomy. The governors and assemblies that governed the legal process in the various colonies of South Africa were launched on a different and independent legislative path from the rest of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>.</p>
<p>In the days of <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a>, slaves required passes to travel away from their masters. In 1797 the <a href="/wiki/Landdrost" title="Landdrost">Landdrost</a> and Heemraden of <a href="/wiki/Swellendam" title="Swellendam">Swellendam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Graaff-Reinet" title="Graaff-Reinet">Graaff-Reinet</a> extended <a href="/wiki/Pass_laws" title="Pass laws">pass laws</a> beyond slaves and ordained that all <a href="/wiki/Khoikhoi" title="Khoikhoi">Khoikhoi</a> (designated as <i>Hottentots</i>) moving about the country for any purpose should carry passes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Afrikaner_political_thought:_analysis_and_documents_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Afrikaner_political_thought:_analysis_and_documents-24">[24]</a></sup> This was confirmed by the British Colonial government in 1809 by the Hottentot Proclamation, which decreed that if a Khoikhoi were to move they would need a pass from their master or a local official.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Afrikaner_political_thought:_analysis_and_documents_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Afrikaner_political_thought:_analysis_and_documents-24">[24]</a></sup> Ordinance No. 49 of 1828 decreed that prospective black immigrants were to be granted passes for the sole purpose of seeking work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Afrikaner_political_thought:_analysis_and_documents_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Afrikaner_political_thought:_analysis_and_documents-24">[24]</a></sup> These passes were to be issued for Coloureds and Khoikhoi, but not for other Africans, who were still forced to carry passes.</p>
<p>The United Kingdom's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavery_Abolition_Act" title="Slavery Abolition Act">Slavery Abolition Act</a> 1833 (3 &amp; 4 Will. IV c. 73) abolished slavery throughout the British Empire and overrode the Cape Articles of Capitulation. To comply with the act the South African legislation was expanded to include Ordinance 1 in 1835, which effectively changed the status of slaves to indentured labourers. This was followed by Ordinance 3 in 1848, which introduced an indenture system for <a href="/wiki/Xhosa_people" title="Xhosa people">Xhosa</a> that was little different from slavery. The various South African colonies passed legislation throughout the rest of the nineteenth century to limit the freedom of unskilled workers, to increase the restrictions on indentured workers and to regulate the relations between the races.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Franchise_and_Ballot_Act" title="Franchise and Ballot Act">Franchise and Ballot Act</a> of 1892 instituted limits based on financial means and education to the black <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">franchise</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and the Natal Legislative Assembly Bill of 1894 deprived Indians of the right to vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glen_Grey_Act" title="Glen Grey Act">Glen Grey Act</a> of 1894, instigated by the government of Prime Minister <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cecil_John_Rhodes" title="Cecil John Rhodes">Cecil John Rhodes</a> limited the amount of land Africans could hold. In 1905 the General Pass Regulations Act denied blacks the vote, limited them to fixed areas and inaugurated the infamous Pass System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The Asiatic Registration Act (1906) required all Indians to register and carry passes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In 1910 the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a> was created as a self-governing <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">dominion</a>, which continued the legislative programme: the South Africa Act (1910) enfranchised whites, giving them complete political control over all other racial groups while removing the right of blacks to sit in parliament,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leach_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-leach-29">[29]</a></sup> the Native Land Act (1913) prevented blacks, except those in the Cape, from buying land outside "reserves",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leach_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-leach-29">[29]</a></sup> the Natives in Urban Areas Bill (1918) was designed to force blacks into "locations",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> the Urban Areas Act (1923) introduced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Residential_segregation" title="Residential segregation">residential segregation</a> and provided cheap labour for industry led by white people, the Colour Bar Act (1926) prevented black mine workers from practising skilled trades, the Native Administration Act (1927) made the British Crown, rather than <a href="/wiki/Paramount_chief" title="Paramount chief">paramount chiefs</a>, the supreme head over all African affairs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (February 2015)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the Native Land and Trust Act (1936) complemented the 1913 Native Land Act and, in the same year, the Representation of Natives Act removed previous black voters from the Cape voters' roll and allowed them to elect three whites to Parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (February 2015)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> One of the first pieces of segregating legislation enacted by <a href="/wiki/Jan_Smuts" title="Jan Smuts">Jan Smuts</a>' <a href="/wiki/United_Party_(South_Africa)" title="United Party (South Africa)">United Party</a> government was the <a href="/wiki/Asiatic_Land_Tenure_and_Indian_Representation_Act,_1946" title="Asiatic Land Tenure and Indian Representation Act, 1946">Asiatic Land Tenure Bill (1946)</a>, which banned land sales to Indians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>The United Party government began to move away from the rigid enforcement of segregationist laws during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Amid fears integration would eventually lead to racial assimilation, the legislature established the <a href="/wiki/Sauer_Commission" title="Sauer Commission">Sauer Commission</a> to investigate the effects of the United Party's policies. The commission concluded that integration would bring about a "loss of personality" for all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(classification_of_human_beings)" title="Race (classification of human beings)">racial groups</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Institution">Institution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Institution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1948">Election of 1948</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Election of 1948">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/South_African_general_election,_1948" title="South African general election, 1948">South African general election, 1948</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DFMalanPortret.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="414" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/DFMalanPortret.jpg/170px-DFMalanPortret.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/DFMalanPortret.jpg/255px-DFMalanPortret.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/DFMalanPortret.jpg/340px-DFMalanPortret.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DFMalanPortret.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Daniel_Fran%C3%A7ois_Malan" title="Daniel François Malan">Daniel François Malan</a>, the first apartheid-era prime minister (1948–1954)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Union of South Africa had allowed social custom and law to govern the consideration of multiracial affairs and of the allocation, in racial terms, of access to economic, social, and political status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zastudy_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-zastudy-35">[35]</a></sup> Most <a href="/wiki/White_South_African" title="White South African">white South Africans</a>, regardless of their own differences, accepted the prevailing pattern. Nevertheless, by 1948 it remained apparent that there were occasional gaps in the social structure, whether legislated or otherwise, concerning the rights and opportunities of nonwhites. The rapid economic development of World War II attracted black migrant workers in large numbers to chief industrial centres, where they compensated for the wartime shortage of white labour. However, this escalated rate of black urbanisation went unrecognised by the South African government, which failed to accommodate the influx with parallel expansion in housing or social services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zastudy_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-zastudy-35">[35]</a></sup> Overcrowding, spiking crime rates, and disillusionment resulted; urban blacks came to support a new generation of leaders influenced by the principles of self-determination and popular freedoms enshrined in such statements as the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Charter" title="Atlantic Charter">Atlantic Charter</a>. Whites reacted negatively to the changes, allowing the <a href="/wiki/Herenigde_Nasionale_Party" title="Herenigde Nasionale Party">Herenigde Nasionale Party</a> (or simply <a href="/wiki/National_Party_(South_Africa)" title="National Party (South Africa)">National Party</a>) to convince a large segment of the voting bloc that the impotence of the United Party in curtailing the evolving position of nonwhites indicated that the organisation had fallen under the influence of Western liberals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zastudy_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-zastudy-35">[35]</a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/Afrikaners" title="Afrikaners">Afrikaners</a>, whites chiefly of <a href="/wiki/Dutch_people" title="Dutch people">Dutch</a> descent but with early infusions of <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">Germans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" title="Huguenot">French Huguenots</a> who were soon assimilated, also resented what they perceived as disempowerment by an underpaid black workforce and the superior economic power and prosperity of white English speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In addition, Jan Smuts, as a strong advocate of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, lost domestic support when South Africa was criticised for its colour bar and continued mandate of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_West_Africa" title="South West Africa">South West Africa</a> by other UN member states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O.27Meara_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-O.27Meara-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Afrikaner nationalists proclaimed that they offered the voters a new policy to ensure continued white domination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meredith_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meredith-38">[38]</a></sup> This policy was initially expounded from a theory drafted by <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Verwoerd" title="Hendrik Verwoerd">Hendrik Verwoerd</a> and was presented to the National Party by the <a href="/wiki/Sauer_Commission" title="Sauer Commission">Sauer Commission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zastudy_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-zastudy-35">[35]</a></sup> It called for a systematic effort to organise the relations, rights, and privileges of the races as officially defined through a series of parliamentary acts and administrative decrees. Segregation had thus been pursued only in major matters, such as separate schools, and local society rather than law had been depended upon to enforce most separation; it should now be extended to everything.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zastudy_35-4"><a href="#cite_note-zastudy-35">[35]</a></sup> The party gave this policy a name – <i>apartheid</i> (apartness). Apartheid was to be the basic ideological and practical foundation of Afrikaner politics for the next quarter of a century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meredith_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Meredith-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>The National Party's election platform stressed that apartheid would preserve a market for white employment in which nonwhites could not compete. On the issues of black urbanisation, the regulation of nonwhite labour, influx control, social security, farm tariffs, and nonwhite taxation the United Party's policy remained contradictory and confused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O.27Meara_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-O.27Meara-37">[37]</a></sup> Its traditional bases of support not only took mutually exclusive positions, but found themselves increasingly at odds with each other. Smuts' reluctance to consider South African foreign policy against the mounting tensions of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> also stirred up discontent, while the nationalists promised to purge the state and public service of communist sympathisers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O.27Meara_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-O.27Meara-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>First to desert the United Party were Afrikaner farmers, who wished to see a change in influx control due to problems with squatters, as well as higher prices for their maize and other produce in the face of the mineowners' demand for cheap food policies. Always identified with the affluent and capitalist, the party also failed to appeal to its working class constituents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O.27Meara_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-O.27Meara-37">[37]</a></sup> Populist rhetoric allowed the National Party to sweep eight constituencies in the mining and industrial centres of the <a href="/wiki/Witwatersrand" title="Witwatersrand">Witwatersrand</a> and five more in <a href="/wiki/Pretoria" title="Pretoria">Pretoria</a>. Barring the predominantly English-speaking landowner electorate of the <a href="/wiki/Natal_Province" title="Natal Province">Natal</a>, the United Party was defeated in almost every rural district. Its urban losses in the nation's most populous province, the <a href="/wiki/Transvaal_Province" title="Transvaal Province">Transvaal</a>, proved equally devastating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O.27Meara_37-4"><a href="#cite_note-O.27Meara-37">[37]</a></sup> As the voting system was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malapportionment" title="Malapportionment">disproportionately weighted</a> in favour of rural constituencies and the Transvaal in particular, the 1948 election catapulted the Herenigde Nasionale Party from a small minority party to a commanding position with an eight-vote parliamentary lead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sahistory-1948election_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-sahistory-1948election-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Fran%C3%A7ois_Malan" title="Daniel François Malan">Daniel François Malan</a> became the first nationalist prime minister, with the aim of implementing the apartheid philosophy and silencing liberal opposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zastudy_35-5"><a href="#cite_note-zastudy-35">[35]</a></sup></p>

<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Apartheid_legislation_in_South_Africa&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>

<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Apartheid_legislation" title="Apartheid legislation">Apartheid legislation</a></div>
<p>NP leaders argued that South Africa did not comprise a single nation, but was made up of four distinct racial groups: white, black, coloured and Indian. Such groups were split into 13 nations or racial federations. White people encompassed the English and <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">Afrikaans</a> language groups; the black populace was divided into ten such groups.</p>
<p>The state passed laws that paved the way for "grand apartheid", which was centred on separating races on a large scale, by compelling people to live in separate places defined by race. This strategy was in part adopted from "left-over" British rule that separated different racial groups after they took control of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boer_republics" title="Boer republics">Boer republics</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Boer_war" title="Anglo-Boer war">Anglo-Boer war</a>. This created the black-only "townships" or "locations", where blacks were relocated to their own towns. In addition, "petty apartheid" laws were passed. The principal apartheid laws were as follows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-b-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first grand apartheid law was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_Registration_Act" title="Population Registration Act">Population Registration Act</a> of 1950, which formalised racial classification and introduced an identity card for all persons over the age of 18, specifying their racial group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Official teams or boards were established to come to a conclusion on those people whose race was unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> This caused difficulty, especially for coloured people, separating their families when members were allocated different races.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>The second pillar of grand apartheid was the <a href="/wiki/Group_Areas_Act" title="Group Areas Act">Group Areas Act</a> of 1950.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Until then, most settlements had people of different races living side by side. This Act put an end to diverse areas and determined where one lived according to race. Each race was allotted its own area, which was used in later years as a basis of forced removal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Prevention_of_Illegal_Squatting_Act,_1951" title="Prevention of Illegal Squatting Act, 1951">Prevention of Illegal Squatting Act</a> of 1951 allowed the government to demolish black <a href="/wiki/Shanty_town" title="Shanty town">shanty town</a> slums and forced white employers to pay for the construction of housing for those black workers who were permitted to reside in cities otherwise reserved for whites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prohibition_of_Mixed_Marriages_Act" title="Prohibition of Mixed Marriages Act">Prohibition of Mixed Marriages Act</a> of 1949 prohibited marriage between persons of different races, and the <a href="/wiki/Immorality_Act" title="Immorality Act">Immorality Act</a> of 1950 made <a href="/wiki/Miscegenation" title="Miscegenation">sexual relations with a person of a different race</a> a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Criminal_offence" title="Criminal offence">criminal offence</a>.</p>
<p>Under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reservation_of_Separate_Amenities_Act" title="Reservation of Separate Amenities Act">Reservation of Separate Amenities Act</a> of 1953, municipal grounds could be reserved for a particular race, creating, among other things, separate beaches, buses, hospitals, schools and universities. Signboards such as "whites only" applied to public areas, even including park benches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Blacks were provided with services greatly inferior to those of whites, and, to a lesser extent, to those of Indian and coloured people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crdi_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-crdi-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Further laws had the aim of suppressing resistance, especially armed resistance, to apartheid. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suppression_of_Communism_Act" title="Suppression of Communism Act">Suppression of Communism Act</a> of 1950 banned any party subscribing to <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">Communism</a>. The act defined Communism and its aims so sweepingly that anyone who opposed government policy risked being labelled as a Communist. Since the law specifically stated that Communism aimed to disrupt racial harmony, it was frequently used to gag opposition to apartheid. Disorderly gatherings were banned, as were certain organisations that were deemed threatening to the government.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Education_in_South_Africa" title="Education in South Africa">Education</a> was segregated by the 1953 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bantu_Education_Act" title="Bantu Education Act">Bantu Education Act</a>, which crafted a separate system of education for black South African students and was designed to prepare black people for lives as a labouring class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-byrnes_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-byrnes-50">[50]</a></sup> In 1959 separate universities were created for black, coloured and Indian people. Existing universities were not permitted to enroll new black students. The Afrikaans Medium Decree of 1974 required the use of <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">Afrikaans</a> and English on an equal basis in high schools outside the homelands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-afrikaans_medium_decree_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-afrikaans_medium_decree-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bantu_Authorities_Act,_1951" title="Bantu Authorities Act, 1951">Bantu Authorities Act of 1951</a> created separate government structures for blacks and whites and was the first piece of legislation to support the government's plan of separate development in the <a href="/wiki/Bantustan" title="Bantustan">bantustans</a>. The Promotion of Black Self-Government Act of 1959 entrenched the NP policy of nominally independent "homelands" for blacks. So-called "self–governing Bantu units" were proposed, which would have devolved administrative powers, with the promise later of <a href="/wiki/Autonomy" title="Autonomy">autonomy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-government" title="Self-government">self-government</a>. It also abolished the seats of white representatives of black South Africans and removed from the rolls the few blacks still qualified to vote. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bantu_Investment_Corporation_Act" title="Bantu Investment Corporation Act">Bantu Investment Corporation Act</a> of 1959 set up a mechanism to transfer capital to the homelands to create employment there. Legislation of 1967 allowed the government to stop industrial development in "white" cities and redirect such development to the "homelands". The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_Homeland_Citizenship_Act" title="Black Homeland Citizenship Act">Black Homeland Citizenship Act</a> of 1970 marked a new phase in the Bantustan strategy. It changed the status of blacks to citizens of one of the ten autonomous territories. The aim was to ensure a demographic majority of white people within South Africa by having all ten Bantustans achieve full independence.</p>
<p>Interracial contact in sport was frowned upon, but there were no segregatory sports laws.</p>
<p>The government tightened pass laws compelling blacks to carry identity documents, to prevent the immigration of blacks from other countries. To reside in a city, blacks had to be in employment there. Until 1956 women were for the most part excluded from these <i>pass</i> requirements, as attempts to introduce <i>pass laws</i> for women were met with fierce resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disenfranchisement_of_Coloured_voters">Disenfranchisement of Coloured voters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Disenfranchisement of Coloured voters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coloured_vote_constitutional_crisis" title="Coloured vote constitutional crisis">Coloured vote constitutional crisis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cape-coloured-children.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="859" data-file-width="1528" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Cape-coloured-children.jpg/220px-Cape-coloured-children.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Cape-coloured-children.jpg/330px-Cape-coloured-children.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Cape-coloured-children.jpg/440px-Cape-coloured-children.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cape-coloured-children.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cape Coloured children in <a href="/wiki/Bonteheuwel" title="Bonteheuwel">Bonteheuwel</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Annual_per_capita_personal_income_by_race_group_in_South_Africa_relative_to_white_levels.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="775" data-file-width="1741" height="98" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Annual_per_capita_personal_income_by_race_group_in_South_Africa_relative_to_white_levels.jpg/220px-Annual_per_capita_personal_income_by_race_group_in_South_Africa_relative_to_white_levels.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Annual_per_capita_personal_income_by_race_group_in_South_Africa_relative_to_white_levels.jpg/330px-Annual_per_capita_personal_income_by_race_group_in_South_Africa_relative_to_white_levels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Annual_per_capita_personal_income_by_race_group_in_South_Africa_relative_to_white_levels.jpg/440px-Annual_per_capita_personal_income_by_race_group_in_South_Africa_relative_to_white_levels.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Annual_per_capita_personal_income_by_race_group_in_South_Africa_relative_to_white_levels.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Annual per capita personal income by race group in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> relative to white levels.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1950, D. F. Malan announced the NP's intention to create a Coloured Affairs Department.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J.G._Strijdom" title="J.G. Strijdom">J.G. Strijdom</a>, Malan's successor as Prime Minister, moved to strip voting rights from black and coloured residents of the Cape Province. The previous government had introduced the Separate Representation of Voters Bill into Parliament in 1951; however, four voters, G Harris, W D Franklin, W D Collins and Edgar Deane, challenged its validity in court with support from the United Party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The Cape Supreme Court upheld the act, but reversed by the Appeal Court, finding the act invalid because a two-thirds majority in a joint sitting of both Houses of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_South_Africa" title="Parliament of South Africa">Parliament</a> was needed to change the <a href="/wiki/Entrenched_clause" title="Entrenched clause">entrenched clauses</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_South_Africa#Previous_constitutions_of_South_Africa" title="Constitution of South Africa">Constitution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The government then introduced the High Court of Parliament Bill (1952), which gave Parliament the power to overrule decisions of the court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The Cape Supreme Court and the Appeal Court declared this invalid too.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1955 the Strijdom government increased the number of judges in the Appeal Court from five to 11, and appointed pro-Nationalist judges to fill the new places.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> In the same year they introduced the Senate Act, which increased the Senate from 49 seats to 89.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Adjustments were made such that the NP controlled 77 of these seats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The parliament met in a joint sitting and passed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Separate_Representation_of_Voters_Act" title="Separate Representation of Voters Act">Separate Representation of Voters Act</a> in 1956, which transferred coloured voters from the common voters' roll in the Cape to a new coloured voters' roll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Immediately after the vote, the Senate was restored to its original size. The Senate Act was contested in the Supreme Court, but the recently enlarged Appeal Court, packed with government-supporting judges, upheld the act, and also the Act to remove coloured voters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 1956 law allowed Coloureds to elect four people to Parliament, but a 1969 law abolished those seats and stripped Coloureds of their right to vote. Since Asians had never been allowed to vote, this resulted in whites being the sole enfranchised group.</p>
<p>A 2016 study in the <a href="/wiki/The_Journal_of_Politics" title="The Journal of Politics">Journal of Politics</a> suggests that disenfranchisement in South Africa had a significant negative impact on basic service delivery to the disenfranchized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Division_among_whites">Division among whites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Division among whites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Before South Africa became a republic, politics among white South Africans was typified by the division between the mainly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afrikaner" title="Afrikaner">Afrikaner</a> pro-republic conservative and the largely English anti-republican liberal sentiments,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> with the legacy of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Boer War</a> still a factor for some people. Once South Africa became a republic, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Verwoerd" title="Hendrik Verwoerd">Hendrik Verwoerd</a> called for improved relations and greater accord between people of British descent and the Afrikaners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> He claimed that the only difference was between those in favor of apartheid and those against it. The ethnic division would no longer be between Afrikaans and English speakers, but between blacks and whites.</p>
<p>Most Afrikaners supported the notion of unanimity of white people to ensure their safety. White voters of British descent were divided. Many had opposed a republic, leading to a majority "no" vote in <a href="/wiki/Natal_Province" title="Natal Province">Natal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Later, some of them recognised the perceived need for white unity, convinced by the growing trend of decolonisation elsewhere in Africa, which concerned them. British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan's</a> "<a href="/wiki/Wind_of_Change_(speech)" title="Wind of Change (speech)">Wind of Change</a>" speech left the British faction feeling that Britain had abandoned them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The more conservative English speakers supported Verwoerd;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> others were troubled by the severing of ties with Britain and remained loyal to the Crown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> They were displeased by having to choose between British and South African nationalities. Although Verwoerd tried to bond these different blocs, the subsequent voting illustrated only a minor swell of support,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> indicating that a great many English speakers remained apathetic and that Verwoerd had not succeeded in uniting the white population.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Homeland_system">Homeland system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Homeland system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bantustan" title="Bantustan">Bantustan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bantustans_in_South_Africa.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="709" data-file-width="809" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Bantustans_in_South_Africa.svg/220px-Bantustans_in_South_Africa.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Bantustans_in_South_Africa.svg/330px-Bantustans_in_South_Africa.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Bantustans_in_South_Africa.svg/440px-Bantustans_in_South_Africa.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bantustans_in_South_Africa.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of South Africa showing the location of bantustans</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ciskei2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="818" data-file-width="1123" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Ciskei2.jpg/220px-Ciskei2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Ciskei2.jpg/330px-Ciskei2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Ciskei2.jpg/440px-Ciskei2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ciskei2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rural area in <a href="/wiki/Ciskei" title="Ciskei">Ciskei</a>, one of the apartheid era <a href="/wiki/Homeland" title="Homeland">homelands</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Under the homeland system, the government attempted to divide South Africa into a number of separate states, each of which was supposed to develop into a separate nation-state for a different ethnic group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Territorial separation was hardly a new institution. There were, for example, the "reserves" created under the British government in the nineteenth century. Under apartheid, 13 percent of the land was reserved for black homelands, a relatively small amount compared with the total population, and generally in economically unproductive areas of the country. The Tomlinson Commission of 1954 justified apartheid and the homeland system, but stated that additional land ought to be given to the homelands, a recommendation that was not carried out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Verwoerd became Prime Minister in 1958, the policy of "separate development" came into being, with the homeland structure as one of its cornerstones. Verwoerd came to believe in the granting of independence to these homelands. The government justified its plans on the basis that "(the) government's policy is, therefore, not a policy of discrimination on the grounds of race or colour, but a policy of differentiation on the ground of nationhood, of different nations, granting to each self-determination within the borders of their homelands – hence this policy of separate development".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Under the homelands system, blacks would no longer be citizens of South Africa, becoming citizens of the independent homelands who worked in South Africa as foreign migrant labourers on temporary work permits. In 1958 the Promotion of Black Self-Government Act was passed, and border industries and the Bantu Investment Corporation were established to promote economic development and the provision of employment in or near the homelands. Many black South Africans who had never resided in their identified homeland were forcibly removed from the cities to the homelands.</p>
<p>Ten homelands were allocated to different black ethnic groups: <a href="/wiki/Lebowa" title="Lebowa">Lebowa</a> (North <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basotho" title="Basotho">Sotho</a>, also referred to as Pedi), <a href="/wiki/QwaQwa" title="QwaQwa">QwaQwa</a> (South Sotho), <a href="/wiki/Bophuthatswana" title="Bophuthatswana">Bophuthatswana</a> (<a href="/wiki/Tswana_people" title="Tswana people">Tswana</a>), <a href="/wiki/KwaZulu" title="KwaZulu">KwaZulu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Zulu_people" title="Zulu people">Zulu</a>), <a href="/wiki/KaNgwane" title="KaNgwane">KaNgwane</a> (<a href="/wiki/Swazi_people" title="Swazi people">Swazi</a>), <a href="/wiki/Transkei" title="Transkei">Transkei</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ciskei" title="Ciskei">Ciskei</a> (<a href="/wiki/Xhosa_people" title="Xhosa people">Xhosa</a>), <a href="/wiki/Gazankulu" title="Gazankulu">Gazankulu</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shangaan" title="Shangaan">Tsonga</a>), <a href="/wiki/Venda" title="Venda">Venda</a> (<a href="/wiki/Venda_people" title="Venda people">Venda</a>) and <a href="/wiki/KwaNdebele" title="KwaNdebele">KwaNdebele</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ndebele_people_(South_Africa)" title="Ndebele people (South Africa)">Ndebele</a>). Four of these were declared independent by the South African government: <a href="/wiki/Transkei" title="Transkei">Transkei</a> in 1976, <a href="/wiki/Bophuthatswana" title="Bophuthatswana">Bophuthatswana</a> in 1977, <a href="/wiki/Venda" title="Venda">Venda</a> in 1979, and <a href="/wiki/Ciskei" title="Ciskei">Ciskei</a> in 1981 (known as the TBVC states). Once a homeland was granted its nominal independence, its designated citizens had their South African citizenship revoked and replaced with citizenship in their homeland. These people were then issued passports instead of passbooks. Citizens of the nominally autonomous homelands also had their South African citizenship circumscribed, meaning they were no longer legally considered South African.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-travel_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-travel-74">[74]</a></sup> The South African government attempted to draw an equivalence between their view of black citizens of the homelands and the problems which other countries faced through entry of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illegal_immigrants" title="Illegal immigrants">illegal immigrants</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_recognition_of_the_Bantustans">International recognition of the Bantustans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: International recognition of the Bantustans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bantustans within the borders of South Africa were classified as "self-governing" or "independent". In theory, self-governing Bantustans had control over many aspects of their internal functioning but were not yet sovereign nations. Independent Bantustans (Transkei, Bophutatswana, Venda and Ciskei; also known as the TBVC states) were intended to be fully sovereign. In reality, they had no significant economic infrastructure and with few exceptions encompassed swaths of disconnected territory. This meant all the Bantustans were little more than puppet states controlled by South Africa.</p>
<p>Throughout the existence of the independent Bantustans, South Africa remained the only country to recognise their independence. Nevertheless, internal organisations of many countries, as well as the South African government, lobbied for their recognition. For example, upon the foundation of Transkei, the Swiss-South African Association encouraged the Swiss government to recognise the new state. In 1976, leading up to a United States House of Representatives resolution urging the President to not recognise Transkei, the South African government intensely lobbied lawmakers to oppose the bill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Each TBVC state extended recognition to the other independent Bantustans while South Africa showed its commitment to the notion of TBVC sovereignty by building embassies in the TBVC capitals.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forced_removals">Forced removals <span id="Forced_removal"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Forced removals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Group_Areas_Act" title="Group Areas Act">Group Areas Act</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abolition_of_Racially_Based_Land_Measures_Act,_1991" title="Abolition of Racially Based Land Measures Act, 1991">Abolition of Racially Based Land Measures Act, 1991</a></div>
<p>During the 1960s, 1970s and early 1980s, the government implemented a policy of "resettlement", to force people to move to their designated "group areas". Millions of people were forced to relocate. These removals included people relocated due to <a href="/wiki/Slum_clearance" title="Slum clearance">slum clearance</a> programmes, labour tenants on white-owned farms, the inhabitants of the so-called "black spots" (black-owned land surrounded by white farms), the families of workers living in townships close to the homelands, and "surplus people" from urban areas, including thousands of people from the Western Cape (which was declared a "Coloured Labour Preference Area")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clp_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-clp-76">[76]</a></sup> who were moved to the <a href="/wiki/Transkei" title="Transkei">Transkei</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ciskei" title="Ciskei">Ciskei</a> homelands. The best-publicised forced removals of the 1950s occurred in <a href="/wiki/Johannesburg" title="Johannesburg">Johannesburg</a>, when 60,000 people were moved to the new township of <a href="/wiki/Soweto" title="Soweto">Soweto</a> (an abbreviation for South Western Townships).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeSophia_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-timeSophia-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>Until 1955, <a href="/wiki/Sophiatown" title="Sophiatown">Sophiatown</a> had been one of the few urban areas where blacks were allowed to own land, and was slowly developing into a multiracial slum. As industry in Johannesburg grew, Sophiatown became the home of a rapidly expanding black workforce, as it was convenient and close to town. It had the only swimming pool for black children in Johannesburg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meredith2010_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meredith2010-79">[79]</a></sup> As one of the oldest black settlements in Johannesburg, it held an almost symbolic importance for the 50,000 blacks it contained, both in terms of its sheer vibrancy and its unique culture.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Tone" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles"><span title="The tone or style of material in the vicinity of this tag may not be appropriate for Wikipedia. (March 2014)">tone</span></a></i>]</sup> Despite a vigorous ANC protest campaign and worldwide publicity, the removal of Sophiatown began on 9 February 1955 under the Western Areas Removal Scheme. In the early hours, heavily armed police forced residents out of their homes and loaded their belongings onto government trucks. The residents were taken to a large tract of land 19 kilometres (12 mi) from the city centre, known as <a href="/wiki/Meadowlands,_Gauteng" title="Meadowlands, Gauteng">Meadowlands</a>, which the government had purchased in 1953. Meadowlands became part of a new planned black city called <a href="/wiki/Soweto" title="Soweto">Soweto</a>. Sophiatown was destroyed by bulldozers, and a new white suburb named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triomf" title="Triomf">Triomf</a> (Triumph) was built in its place. This pattern of forced removal and destruction was to repeat itself over the next few years, and was not limited to black South Africans alone. Forced removals from areas like Cato Manor (Mkhumbane) in <a href="/wiki/Durban" title="Durban">Durban</a>, and <a href="/wiki/District_Six" title="District Six">District Six</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a>, where 55,000 coloured and Indian people were forced to move to new townships on the Cape Flats, were carried out under the <a href="/wiki/Group_Areas_Act" title="Group Areas Act">Group Areas Act</a> of 1950. Nearly 600,000 coloured, Indian and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_South_Africans" title="Chinese South Africans">Chinese people</a> were moved under the Group Areas Act. Some 40,000 whites were also forced to move when land was transferred from "white South Africa" into the black homelands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Petty_apartheid">Petty apartheid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Petty apartheid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
<div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:">Signs enforcing petty apartheid</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg"><img alt="Apartheid sign" data-file-height="609" data-file-width="665" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg/200px-ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg/300px-ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg/400px-ApartheidSignEnglishAfrikaans.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Sign designating a public space as "for use by white persons"</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DurbanSign1989.jpg"><img alt="Apartheid sign" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="587" height="341" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/DurbanSign1989.jpg/200px-DurbanSign1989.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/DurbanSign1989.jpg/300px-DurbanSign1989.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/DurbanSign1989.jpg/400px-DurbanSign1989.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Sign reserving a <a href="/wiki/Natal_Province" title="Natal Province">Natal</a> beach "for the sole use of members of the white race group", in English, <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">Afrikaans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zulu_language" title="Zulu language">Zulu</a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The NP passed a string of legislation that became known as <i>petty apartheid</i>. The first of these was the <a href="/wiki/Prohibition_of_Mixed_Marriages_Act,_1949" title="Prohibition of Mixed Marriages Act, 1949">Prohibition of Mixed Marriages Act 55 of 1949</a>, prohibiting marriage between whites and people of other races. The Immorality Amendment Act 21 of 1950 (as amended in 1957 by Act 23) forbade "unlawful racial intercourse" and "any immoral or indecent act" between a white and a black, Indian or coloured person.</p>
<p>Blacks were not allowed to run businesses or professional practices in areas designated as "white South Africa" unless they had a permit. They were required to move to the black "homelands" and set up businesses and practices there. Transport and civil facilities were segregated. Trains, hospitals and ambulances were segregated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Because of the smaller numbers of white patients and the fact that white doctors preferred to work in white hospitals, conditions in white hospitals were much better than those in often overcrowded and understaffed black hospitals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hospital_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-hospital-82">[82]</a></sup> Blacks were excluded from living or working in white areas, unless they had a pass, nicknamed the <i>dompas</i>, also spelt <i>dompass</i> or <i>dom pass</i>. The most likely origin of this name is from the Afrikaans "verdomde pas" (meaning accursed pass),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dompass_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-dompass-83">[83]</a></sup> although some commentators ascribe it to the Afrikaans words meaning "dumb pass". Only blacks with "Section 10" rights (those who had migrated to the cities before World War II) were excluded from this provision. A pass was issued only to a black with approved work. Spouses and children had to be left behind in black homelands. A pass was issued for one magisterial district (usually one town) confining the holder to that area only. Being without a valid pass made a person subject to arrest and trial for being an illegal migrant. This was often followed by deportation to the person's <a href="/wiki/Bantustan" title="Bantustan">homeland</a> and prosecution of the employer for employing an illegal migrant. Police vans patrolled white areas to round up blacks without passes. Blacks were not allowed to employ whites in white South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although trade unions for black and coloured (mixed race) workers had existed since the early 20th century, it was not until the 1980s reforms that a mass black trade union movement developed. Trade unions under apartheid were racially segregated, with 54 unions being white only, 38 for Indian and coloured and 19 for black people. The Industrial Conciliation Act (1956) legislated against the creation of multi-racial trade unions and attempted to split existing multi-racial unions into separate branches or organisations along racial lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1970s the state spent ten times more per child on the education of white children than on black children within the Bantu Education system (the education system in black schools within white South Africa). Higher education was provided in separate universities and colleges after 1959. Eight black universities were created in the homelands. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_Hare_University" title="Fort Hare University">Fort Hare University</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ciskei" title="Ciskei">Ciskei</a> (now Eastern Cape) was to register only <a href="/wiki/Xhosa_language" title="Xhosa language">Xhosa</a>-speaking students. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sesotho" title="Sesotho">Sotho</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Setswana" title="Setswana">Tswana</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sepedi" title="Sepedi">Pedi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venda_language" title="Venda language">Venda</a> speakers were placed at the newly founded <a href="/wiki/University_of_Limpopo" title="University of Limpopo">University College of the North</a> at Turfloop, while the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Zululand" title="University of Zululand">University College of Zululand</a> was launched to serve Zulu students. Coloureds and Indians were to have their own establishments in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_Western_Cape" title="University of the Western Cape">Cape</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Durban-Westville" title="University of Durban-Westville">Natal</a> respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Each black homeland controlled its own education, health and police systems. Blacks were not allowed to buy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hard_liquor" title="Hard liquor">hard liquor</a>. They were able only to buy state-produced poor quality beer (although this was relaxed later). Public beaches were racially segregated. Public swimming pools, some pedestrian bridges, drive-in cinema parking spaces, graveyards, parks, and public toilets were segregated. Cinemas and theatres in white areas were not allowed to admit blacks. There were practically no cinemas in black areas. Most restaurants and hotels in white areas were not allowed to admit blacks except as staff. Blacks were prohibited from attending white churches under the Churches Native Laws Amendment Act of 1957, but this was never rigidly enforced and churches were one of the few places races could mix without the interference of the law. Blacks earning 360 <a href="/wiki/South_African_rand" title="South African rand">rand</a> a year or more had to pay taxes while the white threshold was more than twice as high, at 750 rand a year. On the other hand, the taxation rate for whites was considerably higher than that for blacks.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Blacks could never acquire land in white areas. In the homelands, much of the land belonged to a "tribe", where the local chieftain would decide how the land had to be used. This resulted in whites owning almost all the industrial and agricultural lands and much of the prized residential land. Most blacks were stripped of their South African citizenship when the "homelands" became "independent", and they were no longer able to apply for South African passports. Eligibility requirements for a passport had been difficult for blacks to meet, the government contending that a passport was a privilege, not a right, and the government did not grant many passports to blacks. Apartheid pervaded culture as well as the law, and was entrenched by most of the mainstream media.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coloured_classification">Coloured classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Coloured classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;padding-top:0.1em;font-style:italic;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Disclogo1.svg"><img alt="Disclogo1.svg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="254" data-file-width="169" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Disclogo1.svg/10px-Disclogo1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Disclogo1.svg/16px-Disclogo1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Disclogo1.svg/21px-Disclogo1.svg.png 2x" width="10"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Discrimination" title="Portal:Discrimination">Discrimination portal</a></td>

<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Discrimination_sidebar" title="Template:Discrimination sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Discrimination_sidebar" title="Template talk:Discrimination sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Discrimination_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
