</table>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coloured" title="Coloured">Coloured</a></div>
<p>The population was classified into four groups: Black, White, Indian, and Coloured (capitalised to denote their legal definitions in South African law). The Coloured group included people regarded as being of mixed descent, including of <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khoisan" title="Khoisan">Khoisan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_ethnic_groups" title="European ethnic groups">European</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malay_race" title="Malay race">Malay</a> ancestry. Many were descended from people brought to South Africa from other parts of the world, such as <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, and China as <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slaves</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indentured_labour" title="Indentured labour">indentured workers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Intro_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Intro-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>The apartheid bureaucracy devised complex (and often arbitrary) criteria at the time that the Population Registration Act was implemented to determine who was Coloured. Minor officials would administer tests to determine if someone should be categorised either Coloured or Black, or if another person should be categorised either Coloured or White. Different members of the same family found themselves in different race groups. Further tests determined membership of the various sub-racial groups of the Coloureds. Many of those who formerly belonged to this racial group are opposed to the continuing use of the term "coloured" in the post-apartheid era, though the term no longer signifies any legal meaning. The expressions "so-called Coloured" (Afrikaans <i>sogenaamde Kleurlinge</i>) and "brown people" (<i>bruinmense</i>) acquired a wide usage in the 1980s.</p>
<p>Discriminated against by apartheid, Coloureds were as a matter of state policy forced to live in separate <a href="/wiki/Township_(South_Africa)" title="Township (South Africa)">townships</a>, in some cases leaving homes their families had occupied for generations, and received an inferior education, though better than that provided to Blacks.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> They played an important role in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-apartheid_movement" title="Anti-apartheid movement">anti-apartheid movement</a>: for example the <a href="/wiki/African_Political_Organization" title="African Political Organization">African Political Organization</a> established in 1902 had an exclusively Coloured membership.</p>
<p>Voting rights were denied to Coloureds in the same way that they were denied to Blacks from 1950 to 1983. However, in 1977 the NP caucus approved proposals to bring Coloureds and Indians into central government. In 1982, final constitutional proposals produced a referendum among Whites, and the <a href="/wiki/Tricameral_Parliament" title="Tricameral Parliament">Tricameral Parliament</a> was approved. The Constitution was reformed the following year to allow the Coloured and Asian minorities participation in separate Houses in a Tricameral Parliament, and Botha became the first Executive State President. The idea was that the Coloured minority could be granted voting rights, but the Black majority were to become citizens of independent homelands. These separate arrangements continued until the abolition of apartheid. The Tricameral reforms led to the formation of the (anti-apartheid) <a href="/wiki/United_Democratic_Front_(South_Africa)" title="United Democratic Front (South Africa)">United Democratic Front</a> as a vehicle to try to prevent the co-option of Coloureds and Indians into an alliance with Whites. The battles between the UDF and the NP government from 1983 to 1989 were to become the most intense period of struggle between left-wing and right-wing South Africans.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Women_under_apartheid">Women under apartheid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Women under apartheid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">Colonialism</a> and apartheid had a major impact on black and coloured women, since they suffered both racial and <a href="/wiki/Sexism" title="Sexism">gender discrimination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Jobs were often hard to find. Many black and coloured women worked as agricultural or domestic workers, but wages were extremely low, if existent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-d-90">[90]</a></sup> Children suffered from diseases caused by malnutrition and sanitation problems, and mortality rates were therefore high. The controlled movement of black and coloured workers within the country through the Natives Urban Areas Act of 1923 and the <a href="/wiki/Pass_laws" title="Pass laws">pass laws</a> separated family members from one another, because men could prove their employment in urban centres while most women were merely dependents; consequently, they risked being deported to rural areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bern_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-bern-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport_under_apartheid">Sport under apartheid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Sport under apartheid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union_and_apartheid" title="Rugby union and apartheid">Rugby union and apartheid</a></div>
<p>By the 1930s, association football mirrored the balkanised society of South Africa; football was divided into numerous institutions based on race: the (White) <a href="/wiki/South_African_Football_Association" title="South African Football Association">South African Football Association</a>, the South African Indian Football Association (SAIFA), the South African African Football Association (SAAFA) and its rival the South African Bantu Football Association, and the South African Coloured Football Association (SACFA). Lack of funds to provide proper equipment would be noticeable in regards to black amateur football matches; this revealed the unequal lives black South Africans were subject to, in contrast to Whites, who were obviously much better off financially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Apartheid's social engineering made it more difficult to compete across racial lines. Thus, in an effort to centralise finances, the federations merged in 1951, creating the South African Soccer Federation (SASF), which brought Black, Indian, and Coloured national associations into one body that opposed apartheid. This was generally opposed more and more by the growing apartheid government, and – with urban segregation being reinforced with ongoing racist policies – it was harder to play football along these racial lines. In 1956, the Pretoria regime – the administrative capital of South Africa – passed the first apartheid sports policy; by doing so, it emphasised the White-led government's opposition to inter-racialism.</p>
<p>While football was plagued by racism, it also played a role in protesting apartheid and its policies. With the international bans from <a href="/wiki/FIFA" title="FIFA">FIFA</a> and other major sporting events, South Africa would be in the spotlight internationally. In a 1977 survey, white South Africans ranked the lack of international sport as one of the three most damaging consequences of apartheid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robnixon_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-robnixon-93">[93]</a></sup> By the mid-1950s, Black South Africans would also use media to challenge the "racialisation" of sports in South Africa; anti-apartheid forces had begun to pinpoint sport as the "weakness" of white national morale. Black journalists for the <i>Johannesburg Drum</i> magazine were the first to give the issue public exposure, with an intrepid special issue in 1955 that asked, "Why shouldn't our blacks be allowed in the SA team?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robnixon_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-robnixon-93">[93]</a></sup> As time progressed, international standing with South Africa would continue to be strained. In the 1980s, as the oppressive system was slowly collapsing the ANC and National Party started negotiations on the end of apartheid. Football associations also discussed the formation of a single, non-racial controlling body. This unity process accelerated in the late 1980s and led to the creation, in December 1991, of an incorporated South African Football Association. On 3 July 1992, FIFA finally welcomed South Africa back into international football.</p>
<p>Sport has long been an important part of life in South Africa, and the boycotting of games by international teams had a profound effect on the white population, perhaps more so than the trade embargoes did. After the re-acceptance of South Africa's sports teams by the international community, sport played a major unifying role between the country's diverse ethnic groups. Mandela's open support of the predominantly white rugby fraternity during the <a href="/wiki/1995_Rugby_World_Cup" title="1995 Rugby World Cup">1995 Rugby World Cup</a> was considered instrumental in bringing together South African sports fans of all races.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Asians_during_apartheid">Asians during apartheid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Asians during apartheid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Indian_South_Africans" title="Indian South Africans">Indian South Africans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asian_South_Africans" title="Asian South Africans">Asian South Africans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_South_Africans" title="Chinese South Africans">Chinese South Africans</a></div>
<p>Defining its Asian population, a minority that did not appear to belong to any of the initial <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> three designated groups, was a constant dilemma for the apartheid government.</p>
<p>For political reasons, the classification of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honorary_white" title="Honorary white">honorary white</a>" was granted to immigrants from <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> – countries with which South Africa maintained diplomatic and economic relations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> – and to their descendants.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Indian_South_Africans" title="Indian South Africans">Indian South Africans</a> during apartheid were classified many ranges of categories from "Asian" to "black" <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> to "Coloured" <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and even the mono-ethnic category of "Indian", but never as white, having been considered "nonwhite" throughout South Africa's history. The group faced severe discrimination during the apartheid regime and were subject to numerous racialist policies.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chinese_South_Africans" title="Chinese South Africans">Chinese South Africans</a> – who were descendants of migrant workers who came to work in the gold mines around Johannesburg in the late 19th century – were initially either classified as "Coloured" or "Other Asian" and were subject to numerous forms of discrimination and restriction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sze_memoir_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sze_memoir-96">[96]</a></sup> It was not until 1984 that South African Chinese, increased to about 10,000, were given the same official rights as the Japanese, to be treated as whites in terms of the Group Areas Act, although they still faced discrimination and did not receive all the benefits/rights of their newly obtained honorary white status such as voting.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Overseas_Indonesians" title="Overseas Indonesians">Indonesians</a> arrived at the Cape of Good Hope as slaves until the abolishment of slavery during the 1800s. They were predominantly Muslim, were allowed religious freedom and formed their own ethnic group/community known as <a href="/wiki/Cape_Malays" title="Cape Malays">Cape Malays</a>. They were classified as part of the Coloured racial group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> This was the same for South Africans of Malaysian descent who were also classified as part of the Coloured race and thus considered "not-white".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Intro_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Intro-87">[87]</a></sup> South Africans of Filipino descent were classified as "black" due to historical outlook on Filipinos by White South Africans, and many of them lived in Bantustans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Intro_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-Intro-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservatism">Conservatism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Conservatism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Alongside apartheid the NP government implemented a programme of <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism" title="Social conservatism">social conservatism</a>. Pornography <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> and gambling<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> were banned. Cinemas, shops selling alcohol and most other businesses were forbidden from operating on Sundays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">Abortion</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apfn.org_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-apfn.org-101">[101]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">homosexuality</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sex_education" title="Sex education">sex education</a> were also restricted; abortion was legal only in cases of rape or if the mother's life was threatened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apfn.org_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-apfn.org-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Television <a href="/wiki/Television_in_South_Africa" title="Television in South Africa">was not introduced</a> until 1976 because the government viewed English programming as a threat to the Afrikaans language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Television was run on apartheid lines – TV1 broadcast in Afrikaans and English (geared to a white audience), TV2 in Zulu and Xhosa and TV3 in Sotho, Tswana and Pedi (both geared to a black audience), and TV4 mostly showed programmes for an urban-black audience.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_resistance">Internal resistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Internal resistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Internal_resistance_to_apartheid" title="Internal resistance to apartheid">Internal resistance to apartheid</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Murder_at_Sharpeville_21_March_1960.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="2018" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Murder_at_Sharpeville_21_March_1960.jpg/220px-Murder_at_Sharpeville_21_March_1960.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Murder_at_Sharpeville_21_March_1960.jpg/330px-Murder_at_Sharpeville_21_March_1960.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Murder_at_Sharpeville_21_March_1960.jpg/440px-Murder_at_Sharpeville_21_March_1960.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Murder_at_Sharpeville_21_March_1960.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Painting of the Sharpeville Massacre of March 1960</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Apartheid sparked significant internal resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lodge_1983_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lodge_1983-15">[15]</a></sup> The government responded to a series of popular uprisings and protests with police brutality, which in turn increased local support for the armed resistance struggle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANC_Armed_Struggle_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-ANC_Armed_Struggle-104">[104]</a></sup> Internal resistance to the apartheid system in South Africa came from several sectors of society and saw the creation of organisations dedicated variously to peaceful protests, passive resistance and armed insurrection.</p>
<p>In 1949, the <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress_Youth_League" title="African National Congress Youth League">youth wing</a> of the <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress" title="African National Congress">African National Congress</a> (ANC) took control of the organisation and started advocating a radical black nationalist programme. The new young leaders proposed that white authority could only be overthrown through mass campaigns. In 1950 that philosophy saw the launch of the Programme of Action, a series of strikes, boycotts and civil disobedience actions that led to occasional violent clashes with the authorities.</p>
<p>In 1959, a group of disenchanted ANC members formed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pan_Africanist_Congress" title="Pan Africanist Congress">Pan Africanist Congress</a> (PAC), which organised a demonstration against pass books on 21 March 1960. One of those protests was held in the township of <a href="/wiki/Sharpeville" title="Sharpeville">Sharpeville</a>, where 69 people were killed by police in the <a href="/wiki/Sharpeville_massacre" title="Sharpeville massacre">Sharpeville massacre</a>.</p>
<p>In the wake of Sharpeville, the government declared a state of emergency. More than 18,000 people were arrested, including leaders of the ANC and PAC, and both organisations were banned. The resistance went underground, with some leaders in exile abroad and others engaged in campaigns of <a href="/wiki/Sabotage" title="Sabotage">domestic sabotage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terrorism" title="Terrorism">terrorism</a>.</p>
<p>In May 1961, before the declaration of South Africa as a Republic, an assembly representing the banned ANC called for negotiations between the members of the different ethnic groupings, threatening demonstrations and strikes during the inauguration of the Republic if their calls were ignored.</p>
<p>When the government overlooked them, the strikers (among the main organisers was a 42-year-old, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thembu" title="Thembu">Thembu</a>-origin <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a>) carried out their threats. The government countered swiftly by giving police the authority to arrest people for up to twelve days and detaining many strike leaders amid numerous cases of police brutality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Defeated, the protesters called off their strike. The ANC then chose to launch an armed struggle through a newly formed military wing, <a href="/wiki/Umkhonto_we_Sizwe" title="Umkhonto we Sizwe">Umkhonto we Sizwe</a> (MK), which would perform acts of sabotage on tactical state structures. Its first sabotage plans were carried out on 16 December 1961, the anniversary of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Blood_River" title="Battle of Blood River">Battle of Blood River</a>.</p>
<p>In the 1970s, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Consciousness_Movement" title="Black Consciousness Movement">Black Consciousness Movement</a> was created by tertiary students influenced by the American Black Power movement. BC endorsed black pride and African customs and did much to alter the feelings of inadequacy instilled among black people by the apartheid system. The leader of the movement, <a href="/wiki/Steve_Biko" title="Steve Biko">Steve Biko</a>, was taken into custody on 18 August 1977 and was beaten to death in detention.</p>
<p>In 1976, secondary students in Soweto took to the streets in the <a href="/wiki/Soweto_uprising" title="Soweto uprising">Soweto uprising</a> to protest against the imposition of Afrikaans as the only language of instruction. On 16 June, police opened fire on students protesting peacefully. According to official reports 23 people were killed, but the number of people who died is usually given as 176, with estimates of up to 700.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrison1987_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison1987-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> In the following years several student organisations were formed to protest against apartheid, and these organisations were central to urban school boycotts in 1980 and 1983 and rural boycotts in 1985 and 1986.</p>
<p>In parallel with student protests, labour unions started protest action in 1973 and 1974. After 1976 unions and workers are considered to have played an important role in the struggle against apartheid, filling the gap left by the banning of political parties. In 1979 black trade unions were legalised and could engage in collective bargaining, although strikes were still illegal. Economist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Sowell" title="Thomas Sowell">Thomas Sowell</a> wrote that basic <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">supply and demand</a> led to violations of Apartheid "on a massive scale" throughout the nation, simply because there were not enough white South African business owners to meet the demand for various goods and services. Large portions of the garment industry and construction of new homes, for example, were effectively owned and operated by blacks, who either worked surreptitiously or who circumvented the law with a white person as a nominal, figurehead manager.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1983, anti-apartheid leaders determined to resist the tricameral parliament assembled to form the <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/United_Democratic_Front" title="United Democratic Front">United Democratic Front</a> (UDF) in order to coordinate anti-apartheid activism inside South Africa. The first presidents of the UDF were <a href="/wiki/Archie_Gumede" title="Archie Gumede">Archie Gumede</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Mpetha" title="Oscar Mpetha">Oscar Mpetha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albertina_Sisulu" title="Albertina Sisulu">Albertina Sisulu</a>; patrons were Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Desmond_Tutu" title="Desmond Tutu">Desmond Tutu</a>, Dr <a href="/wiki/Allan_Boesak" title="Allan Boesak">Allan Boesak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helen_Joseph" title="Helen Joseph">Helen Joseph</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a>. Basing its platform on abolishing apartheid and creating a nonracial democratic South Africa, the UDF provided a legal way for domestic human rights groups and individuals of all races to organise demonstrations and campaign against apartheid inside the country. Churches and church groups also emerged as pivotal points of resistance. Church leaders were not immune to prosecution, and certain faith-based organisations were banned, but the clergy generally had more freedom to criticise the government than militant groups did. The UDF, coupled with the protection of the church, accordingly permitted a major role for Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Desmond_Tutu" title="Desmond Tutu">Desmond Tutu</a>, who served both as a prominent domestic voice and international spokesperson denouncing apartheid and urging the creation of a shared nonracial state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the majority of whites supported apartheid, some 20 percent did not. Parliamentary opposition was galvanised by <a href="/wiki/Helen_Suzman" title="Helen Suzman">Helen Suzman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colin_Eglin" title="Colin Eglin">Colin Eglin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harry_Schwarz" title="Harry Schwarz">Harry Schwarz</a>, who formed the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Federal_Party" title="Progressive Federal Party">Progressive Federal Party</a>. Extra-parliamentary resistance was largely centred in the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Communist_Party" title="South African Communist Party">South African Communist Party</a> and women's organisation the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sash" title="Black Sash">Black Sash</a>. Women were also notable in their involvement in trade union organisations and banned political parties.</p>

<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_South_Africa_during_apartheid" title="Foreign relations of South Africa during apartheid">Foreign relations of South Africa during apartheid</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commonwealth">Commonwealth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Commonwealth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>South Africa's policies were subject to international scrutiny in 1960, when Macmillan criticised them during his celebrated <a href="/wiki/Wind_of_Change_(speech)" title="Wind of Change (speech)">Wind of Change</a> speech in Cape Town. Weeks later, tensions came to a head in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharpeville_Massacre" title="Sharpeville Massacre">Sharpeville Massacre</a>, resulting in more international condemnation. Soon afterwards Verwoerd announced a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1960_South_Africa_referendum" title="1960 South Africa referendum">referendum</a> on whether the country should become a republic. Verwoerd lowered the voting age for whites to 18 and included whites in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_West_Africa" title="South West Africa">South West Africa</a> on the roll. The referendum on 5 October that year asked whites, "Are you in favour of a Republic for the Union?", and 52 percent voted "Yes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a consequence of this change of status, South Africa needed to reapply for continued membership of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a>, with which it had privileged trade links. India had become a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_within_the_Commonwealth" title="Republic within the Commonwealth">republic within the Commonwealth</a> in 1950, but it became clear that African and Asian member states would oppose South Africa due to its apartheid policies. As a result, South Africa withdrew from the Commonwealth on 31 May 1961, the day that the Republic came into existence.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Nations">United Nations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: United Nations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We stand here today to salute the United Nations Organisation and its Member States, both singly and collectively, for joining forces with the masses of our people in a common struggle that has brought about our emancipation and pushed back the frontiers of racism.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a>, address to the United Nations as South African President, 3 October 1994<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>At the first UN gathering in 1946, South Africa was placed on the agenda. The primary subject in question was the handling of South African Indians, a great cause of divergence between South Africa and India. In 1952, apartheid was again discussed in the aftermath of the Defiance Campaign, and the UN set up a task team to keep watch on the progress of apartheid and the racial state of affairs in South Africa. Although South Africa's racial policies were a cause for concern, most countries in the UN concurred that this was a domestic affair, which fell outside the UN's jurisdiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 1960, the UN's conservative stance on apartheid changed following the <a href="/wiki/Sharpeville_massacre" title="Sharpeville massacre">Sharpeville massacre</a>, and the Security Council for the first time agreed on concerted action against the apartheid regime, demanding an end to racial separation and discrimination. From 1960 the ANC began a campaign of armed struggle of which there would later be a charge of 193 acts of terrorism from 1961 to 1963, mainly bombings and murders of civilians.</p>
<p>Instead, the South African government began further suppression, banning the ANC and PAC. In 1961, UN Secretary-General <a href="/wiki/Dag_Hammarskj%C3%B6ld" title="Dag Hammarskjöld">Dag Hammarskjöld</a> stopped over in South Africa and subsequently stated that he had been unable to reach agreement with Prime Minister Verwoerd.</p>
<p>In 1961, dismissing an Israeli vote against South African apartheid at the United Nations, Verwoerd famously said, "Israel is not consistent in its new anti-apartheid attitude … they took Israel away from the Arabs after the Arabs lived there for a thousand years. In that, I agree with them. Israel, like South Africa, is an apartheid state."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 6 November 1962, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> passed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_General_Assembly_Resolution_1761" title="UN General Assembly Resolution 1761">Resolution 1761</a>, condemning apartheid policies. In 1966, the UN held the first of many colloquiums on apartheid. The General Assembly announced 21 March as the International Day for the Elimination of Racial Discrimination, in memory of the Sharpeville massacre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> In 1971, the General Assembly formally denounced the institution of homelands, and a motion was passed in 1974 to expel South Africa from the UN, but this was vetoed by France, the United Kingdom and the United States, all key trade associates of South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 7 August 1963 the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a> passed <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_181" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 181">Resolution 181</a>, calling for a voluntary <a href="/wiki/Arms_embargo" title="Arms embargo">arms embargo</a> against South Africa. In the same year a Special Committee Against Apartheid was established to encourage and oversee plans of action against the regime. From 1964 the US and Britain discontinued their arms trade with South Africa. The Security Council also condemned the Soweto massacre in <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_392" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 392">Resolution 392</a>. In 1977, the voluntary UN arms embargo became mandatory with the passing of <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_418" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 418">Resolution 418</a>.</p>
<p>Economic sanctions against South Africa were also frequently debated as an effective way of putting pressure on the apartheid government. In 1962, the UN General Assembly requested that its members sever political, fiscal and transportation ties with South Africa. In 1968, it proposed ending all cultural, educational and sporting connections as well. Economic sanctions, however, were not made mandatory, because of opposition from South Africa's main trading partners.</p>
<p>In 1973, the UN adopted the Apartheid Convention which defines apartheid and even qualifies it as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crime_against_humanity" title="Crime against humanity">crime against humanity</a> which might lead to international criminal prosecution of the individuals responsible for perpetrating it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> This convention has however only been ratified by 107 of the 193 member states as of August 2008. The convention was initially drafted by the former USSR and Guinea, before being presented to the UN General Assembly. The convention was adopted with a vote of 91 for, and 4 (Portugal, South Africa, the United Kingdom and the United States) against the convention.</p>
<p>In 1978 and 1983 the UN condemned South Africa at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Conference_Against_Racism" title="World Conference Against Racism">World Conference Against Racism</a>.</p>
<p>After much debate, by the late 1980s the United States, the United Kingdom, and 23 other nations had passed laws placing various trade sanctions on South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> A disinvestment from South Africa movement in many countries was similarly widespread, with individual cities and provinces around the world implementing various laws and local regulations forbidding registered corporations under their jurisdiction from doing business with South African firms, factories, or banks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholic_Church">Catholic Church</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Catholic Church">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II" title="Pope John Paul II">Pope John Paul II</a> was an outspoken opponent of apartheid. In 1985, while visiting the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, he gave an impassioned speech at the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a> condemning apartheid, proclaiming that "no system of apartheid or separate development will ever be acceptable as a model for the relations between peoples or races."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> In September 1988 he made a pilgrimage to countries bordering South Africa, while demonstratively avoiding South Africa itself. During his visit to <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>, he called for economic sanctions against South Africa's government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organisation_for_African_Unity">Organisation for African Unity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Organisation for African Unity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Lusaka_Manifesto" title="Lusaka Manifesto">Lusaka Manifesto</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_African_Unity" title="Organisation of African Unity">Organisation of African Unity</a> (OAU) was created in 1963. Its primary objectives were to eradicate colonialism and improve social, political and economic situations in Africa. It censured apartheid and demanded sanctions against South Africa. African states agreed to aid the liberation movements in their fight against apartheid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geld_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-geld-122">[122]</a></sup> In 1969, fourteen nations from Central and East Africa gathered in <a href="/wiki/Lusaka" title="Lusaka">Lusaka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a>, and formulated the <i><a href="/wiki/Lusaka_Manifesto" title="Lusaka Manifesto">Lusaka Manifesto</a></i>, which was signed on 13 April by all of the countries in attendance except <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> This manifesto was later taken on by both the OAU and the United Nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geld_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-geld-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lusaka_Manifesto" title="Lusaka Manifesto">Lusaka Manifesto</a> summarised the political situations of self-governing African countries, condemning racism and inequity, and calling for black majority rule in all African nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> It did not rebuff South Africa entirely, though, adopting an appeasing manner towards the apartheid government, and even recognising its autonomy. Although African leaders supported the emancipation of black South Africans, they preferred this to be attained through peaceful means.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>South Africa's negative response to the Lusaka Manifesto and rejection of a change to its policies brought about another OAU announcement in October 1971. The <i>Mogadishu Declaration</i> stated that South Africa's rebuffing of negotiations meant that its black people could only be freed through military means, and that no African state should converse with the apartheid government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outward-looking_policy">Outward-looking policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Outward-looking policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1966 <a href="/wiki/B._J._Vorster" title="B. J. Vorster">B. J. Vorster</a> became Prime Minister. He was not prepared to dismantle apartheid, but he did try to redress South Africa's isolation and to revitalise the country's global reputation, even those with black-ruled nations in Africa. This he called his "Outward-Looking" policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>Vorster's willingness to talk to African leaders stood in contrast to Verwoerd's refusal to engage with leaders such as <a href="/wiki/Abubakar_Tafawa_Balewa" title="Abubakar Tafawa Balewa">Abubakar Tafawa Balewa</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> in 1962 and <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Kaunda" title="Kenneth Kaunda">Kenneth Kaunda</a> of <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> in 1964. In 1966, he met the heads of the neighbouring states of <a href="/wiki/Lesotho" title="Lesotho">Lesotho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swaziland" title="Swaziland">Swaziland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a>. In 1967, he offered technological and financial aid to any African state prepared to receive it, asserting that no political strings were attached, aware that many African states needed financial aid despite their opposition to South Africa's racial policies. Many were also tied to South Africa economically because of their migrant labour population working on the South African mines. Botswana, Lesotho and Swaziland remained outspoken critics of apartheid, but depended on South Africa's economic aid.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a> was the first country not on South African borders to accept South African aid. In 1967, the two states set out their political and economic relations, and, in 1969, Malawi became the only country at the assembly which did not sign the Lusaka Manifesto condemning South Africa' apartheid policy. In 1970, Malawian president <a href="/wiki/Hastings_Banda" title="Hastings Banda">Hastings Banda</a> made his first and most successful official stopover in South Africa.</p>
<p>Associations with Mozambique followed suit and were sustained after that country won its sovereignty in 1975. Angola was also granted South African loans. Other countries which formed relationships with South Africa were <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a>, Madagascar, <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a>, Gabon, <a href="/wiki/Zaire" title="Zaire">Zaire</a> (now the Democratic Republic of the Congo) and the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a>. Although these states condemned apartheid (more than ever after South Africa's denunciation of the Lusaka Manifesto), South Africa's economic and military dominance meant that they remained dependent on South Africa to varying degrees<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports_and_culture">Sports and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Sports and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sporting_boycott_of_South_Africa" title="Sporting boycott of South Africa">Sporting boycott of South Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union_and_apartheid" title="Rugby union and apartheid">Rugby union and apartheid</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Beginning">Beginning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Beginning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>South Africa's isolation in sport began in the mid-1950s and increased throughout the 1960s. Apartheid forbade multiracial sport, which meant that overseas teams, by virtue of their having players of diverse races, could not play in South Africa. In 1956, the <a href="/wiki/International_Table_Tennis_Federation" title="International Table Tennis Federation">International Table Tennis Federation</a> severed its ties with the all-white South African Table Tennis Union, preferring the non-racial South African Table Tennis Board. The apartheid government responded by confiscating the passports of the Board's players so that they were unable to attend international games.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Isolation">Isolation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Isolation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Verwoerd_years">Verwoerd years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Verwoerd years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In 1959, the non-racial South African Sports Association (SASA) was formed to secure the rights of all players on the global field. After meeting with no success in its endeavours to attain credit by collaborating with white establishments, SASA approached the <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">International Olympic Committee</a> (IOC) in 1962, calling for South Africa's expulsion from the Olympic Games. The IOC sent South Africa a caution to the effect that, if there were no changes, they would be barred from the <a href="/wiki/1964_Summer_Olympics" title="1964 Summer Olympics">1964 Olympic Games</a>. The changes were initiated, and in January 1963, the South African Non-Racial Olympic Committee (SANROC) was set up. The Anti-Apartheid Movement persisted in its campaign for South Africa's exclusion, and the IOC acceded in barring the country from the 1964 Games in Tokyo. South Africa selected a multi-racial team for the next Games, and the IOC opted for incorporation in the <a href="/wiki/1968_Summer_Olympics" title="1968 Summer Olympics">1968 Games in Mexico</a>. Because of protests from AAMs and African nations, however, the IOC was forced to retract the invitation.</p>
<p>Foreign complaints about South Africa's bigoted sports brought more isolation. Racially selected New Zealand sports teams toured South Africa, until the 1970 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All_Blacks" title="All Blacks">All Blacks</a> rugby tour allowed <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_people" title="Māori people">Maori</a> to go under the status of "honorary whites". Huge and widespread protests occurred in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1981_South_Africa_rugby_union_tour_of_New_Zealand" title="1981 South Africa rugby union tour of New Zealand">New Zealand in 1981</a> against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Springboks" title="Springboks">Springbok</a> tour – the government spent $8 million protecting games using the army and police force. A planned All Black tour to South Africa in 1985 remobilised the New Zealand protesters and it was cancelled. A "rebel tour" – not government sanctioned – went ahead in 1986, but after that sporting ties were cut, and New Zealand made a decision not to convey an authorised rugby team to South Africa until the end of apartheid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Vorster_years">Vorster years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Vorster years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Vorster replaced Verwoerd as Prime Minister in 1966 following the latter's assassination and declared that South Africa would no longer dictate to other countries what their teams should look like. Although this reopened the gate for international sporting meets, it did not signal the end of South Africa's racist sporting policies. In 1968 Vorster went against his policy by refusing to permit <a href="/wiki/Basil_D%27Oliveira" title="Basil D'Oliveira">Basil D'Oliveira</a>, a Coloured South African-born cricketer, to join the English cricket team on its tour to South Africa. Vorster said that the side had been chosen only to prove a point, and not on merit. After protests, however, "Dolly" was eventually included in the team. Protests against certain tours brought about the cancellation of a number of other visits, including that of an England rugby team touring South Africa in 1969/70.</p>
<p>The first of the "White Bans" occurred in 1971 when the Chairman of the Australian Cricketing Association – <a href="/wiki/Don_Bradman" title="Don Bradman">Sir Don Bradman</a> – flew to South Africa to meet Vorster. Vorster had expected Bradman to allow the tour of the Australian cricket team to go ahead, but things became heated after Bradman asked why black sportsmen were not allowed to play cricket. Vorster stated that blacks were intellectually inferior and had no finesse for the game. Bradman – thinking this ignorant and repugnant – asked Vorster if he had heard of a man named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garry_Sobers" title="Garry Sobers">Garry Sobers</a>. On his return to Australia, Bradman released a one sentence statement: "We will not play them until they choose a team on a non-racist basis."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>In South Africa, Vorster vented his anger publicly against Bradman, while the African National Congress rejoiced. This was the first time a predominantly white nation had taken the side of multiracial sport, producing an unsettling resonance that more "White" boycotts were coming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Almost twenty years later, on his release from prison, Nelson Mandela asked a visiting Australian statesman if Donald Bradman, his childhood hero, was still alive (Bradman lived until 2001).</p>
<p>In 1971, Vorster altered his policies even further by distinguishing multiracial from multinational sport. Multiracial sport, between teams with players of different races, remained outlawed; multinational sport, however, was now acceptable: international sides would not be subject to South Africa's racial stipulations.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/1978_Commonwealth_Games" title="1978 Commonwealth Games">1978</a>, Nigeria <a href="/wiki/Boycott" title="Boycott">boycotted</a> the Commonwealth Games because New Zealand's sporting contacts with the South African government were not considered to be in accordance with the 1977 <a href="/wiki/Gleneagles_Agreement" title="Gleneagles Agreement">Gleneagles Agreement</a>. Nigeria also led the 32-nation boycott of the <a href="/wiki/1986_Commonwealth_Games" title="1986 Commonwealth Games">1986 Commonwealth Games</a> because of British prime minister <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher's</a> ambivalent attitude towards sporting links with South Africa, significantly affecting the quality and profitability of the Games and thus thrusting apartheid into the international spotlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_boycott">Cultural boycott</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Cultural boycott">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the 1960s, the Anti-Apartheid Movements began to campaign for <i>cultural</i> boycotts of apartheid South Africa. Artists were requested not to present or let their works be hosted in South Africa. In 1963, 45 British writers put their signatures to an affirmation approving of the boycott, and, in 1964, American actor <a href="/wiki/Marlon_Brando" title="Marlon Brando">Marlon Brando</a> called for a similar affirmation for films. In 1965, the <a href="/wiki/Writers%27_Guild_of_Great_Britain" title="Writers' Guild of Great Britain">Writers' Guild of Great Britain</a> called for a proscription on the sending of films to South Africa. Over sixty American artists signed a statement against apartheid and against professional links with the state. The presentation of some South African plays in Britain and the United States was also vetoed. After the arrival of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TV" title="TV">television</a> in South Africa in 1975, the British Actors Union, <a href="/wiki/Equity_(trade_union)" title="Equity (trade union)">Equity</a>, boycotted the service, and no British programme concerning its associates could be sold to South Africa. Sporting and cultural boycotts did not have the same impact as economic sanctions,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> but they did much to lift consciousness amongst normal South Africans of the global condemnation of apartheid.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_influence">Western influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Western influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boycott_Apartheid_Bus,_London,_UK._1989.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="633" data-file-width="1024" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Boycott_Apartheid_Bus%2C_London%2C_UK._1989.jpg/220px-Boycott_Apartheid_Bus%2C_London%2C_UK._1989.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Boycott_Apartheid_Bus%2C_London%2C_UK._1989.jpg/330px-Boycott_Apartheid_Bus%2C_London%2C_UK._1989.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Boycott_Apartheid_Bus%2C_London%2C_UK._1989.jpg/440px-Boycott_Apartheid_Bus%2C_London%2C_UK._1989.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boycott_Apartheid_Bus,_London,_UK._1989.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
London "Boycott Apartheid" bus, 1989</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While international opposition to apartheid grew, the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic countries</a> – and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> in particular – provided both moral and financial support for the <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress" title="African National Congress">ANC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> On 21 February 1986 – a week before he was murdered – <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden's</a> prime minister <a href="/wiki/Olof_Palme" title="Olof Palme">Olof Palme</a> made the <a href="/wiki/Keynote" title="Keynote">keynote</a> address to the <i>Swedish People's Parliament Against Apartheid</i> held in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> In addressing the hundreds of anti-apartheid sympathisers as well as leaders and officials from the ANC and the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Apartheid_Movement" title="Anti-Apartheid Movement">Anti-Apartheid Movement</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Tambo" title="Oliver Tambo">Oliver Tambo</a>, Palme declared: "Apartheid cannot be reformed; it has to be eliminated."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other Western countries adopted a more ambivalent position. In <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swiss-South_African_Association" title="Swiss-South African Association">Swiss-South African Association</a> lobbied on behalf of the South African government. In the 1980s, the US <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Reagan</a> and UK Thatcher administrations followed a "<a href="/wiki/Constructive_engagement" title="Constructive engagement">constructive engagement</a>" policy with the apartheid government, vetoing the imposition of UN economic sanctions, justified by a belief in free trade and a vision of South Africa as a bastion against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marxist" title="Marxist">Marxist</a> forces in Southern Africa. Thatcher declared the ANC a terrorist organisation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> and in 1987 her spokesman, <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Ingham" title="Bernard Ingham">Bernard Ingham</a>, famously said that anyone who believed that the ANC would ever form the government of South Africa was "living in <a href="/wiki/Cloud_cuckoo_land" title="Cloud cuckoo land">cloud cuckoo land</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Legislative_Exchange_Council" title="American Legislative Exchange Council">American Legislative Exchange Council</a> (ALEC), a conservative lobbying organisation, actively campaigned against divesting from South Africa throughout the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the late 1980s, with the tide of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> turning and no sign of a political resolution in South Africa, Western patience began to run out. By 1989, a bipartisan <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a>/<a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic</a> initiative in the US favoured <a href="/wiki/Disinvestment_from_South_Africa" title="Disinvestment from South Africa">economic sanctions</a> (realised as the <i><a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_Anti-Apartheid_Act" title="Comprehensive Anti-Apartheid Act">Comprehensive Anti-Apartheid Act</a></i> of 1986), the release of Nelson Mandela and a negotiated settlement involving the ANC. Thatcher too began to take a similar line, but insisted on the suspension of the ANC's armed struggle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>Britain's significant economic involvement in South Africa may have provided some <a href="/wiki/Leverage_(negotiation)" title="Leverage (negotiation)">leverage</a> with the South African government, with both the UK and the US applying pressure and pushing for negotiations. However, neither Britain nor the US was willing to apply economic pressure upon their multinational interests in South Africa, such as the mining company <a href="/wiki/Anglo_American_plc" title="Anglo American plc">Anglo American</a>. Although a high-profile compensation claim against these companies was thrown out of court in 2004,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Supreme_Court" title="US Supreme Court">US Supreme Court</a> in May 2008 upheld an appeal court ruling allowing another lawsuit that seeks damages of more than US$400 billion from major international companies which are accused of aiding South Africa's apartheid system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Cold_War_and_Total_Onslaught">The Cold War and Total Onslaught</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: The Cold War and Total Onslaught">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SADF_Pamphlet1.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="656" data-file-width="594" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/SADF_Pamphlet1.PNG/220px-SADF_Pamphlet1.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/SADF_Pamphlet1.PNG/330px-SADF_Pamphlet1.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/SADF_Pamphlet1.PNG/440px-SADF_Pamphlet1.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SADF_Pamphlet1.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Propaganda leaflet issued to South African military personnel in the 1980s. The pamphlet decries "Russian colonialism and oppression".</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the 1950s, South African military strategy was decisively shaped by fears of communist espionage and a conventional Soviet threat to the strategic Cape trade route between the south Atlantic and Indian Oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berridge_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berridge-143">[143]</a></sup> The apartheid government supported the U.S.-led <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty_Organization" title="North Atlantic Treaty Organization">North Atlantic Treaty Organization</a> (NATO), as well as its policy of regional <i><a href="/wiki/Containment" title="Containment">containment</a></i> against Soviet-backed regimes and insurgencies worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lulat_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lulat-144">[144]</a></sup> By the late 1960s, the rise of Soviet client states on the African continent, as well as Soviet aid for militant anti-apartheid movements, was considered one of the primary external threats to the apartheid system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-145">[145]</a></sup> South African officials frequently accused domestic opposition groups of being communist proxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Devils_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Devils-146">[146]</a></sup> For its part the Soviet Union viewed South Africa as a bastion of <a href="/wiki/Neocolonialism" title="Neocolonialism">neocolonialism</a> and a regional Western ally, which helped fuel its support for various anti-apartheid causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shultz_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shultz-147">[147]</a></sup> From 1973 onward much of South Africa's white population increasingly looked upon their country as a bastion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_world" title="Free world">free world</a> besieged militarily, politically, and culturally by communism and radical black nationalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Onslaught1_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Onslaught1-148">[148]</a></sup> The apartheid government perceived itself as being locked in a proxy struggle with the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a> and by implication, armed wings of black nationalist forces such as <a href="/wiki/Umkhonto_we_Sizwe" title="Umkhonto we Sizwe">Umkhonto we Sizwe</a> (MK) and the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Liberation_Army_of_Namibia" title="People's Liberation Army of Namibia">People's Liberation Army of Namibia</a> (PLAN), which often received Soviet arms and training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shultz_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shultz-147">[147]</a></sup> This was described as "Total Onslaught".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Onslaught1_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-Onslaught1-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minter_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Minter-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>South African initiatives designed to counter "Total Onslaught" were known as "Total Strategy" and involved building up a formidable conventional military and counter-intelligence capability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Onslaught1_148-2"><a href="#cite_note-Onslaught1-148">[148]</a></sup> Total Strategy was built on the principles of counter-revolution as espoused by noted French tactician <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Beaufre" title="André Beaufre">André Beaufre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minter_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Minter-149">[149]</a></sup> Considerable effort was devoted towards circumventing <a href="/wiki/International_sanctions" title="International sanctions">international arms sanctions</a>, and the government even went so far as to develop <a href="/wiki/South_Africa_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="South Africa and weapons of mass destruction">nuclear weapons</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> allegedly with covert assistance from <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> In 2010, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> released South African government documents that revealed an Israeli offer to sell the apartheid regime nuclear weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Israel categorically denied these allegations and claimed that the documents were minutes from a meeting which did not indicate any concrete offer for a sale of nuclear weapons. <a href="/wiki/Shimon_Peres" title="Shimon Peres">Shimon Peres</a> said that <i>The Guardian</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s article was based on "selective interpretation... and not on concrete facts."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>From the late 1970s to the late 1980s, defence budgets in South Africa were raised exponentially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minter_149-2"><a href="#cite_note-Minter-149">[149]</a></sup> Covert operations focused on espionage and domestic political manipulation became common, the number of <a href="/wiki/Special_forces" title="Special forces">special forces</a> units swelled, and the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Defence_Force" title="South African Defence Force">South African Defence Force</a> had amassed enough sophisticated conventional weaponry to pose a serious threat to the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Front-line_states" title="Front-line states">front-line states</a>", a regional alliance of neighbouring countries opposed to apartheid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minter_149-3"><a href="#cite_note-Minter-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_military_operations">Foreign military operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Foreign military operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/South_African_Border_War" title="South African Border War">South African Border War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SADF-Operations_4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="485" data-file-width="604" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/SADF-Operations_4.jpg/220px-SADF-Operations_4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/SADF-Operations_4.jpg/330px-SADF-Operations_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/SADF-Operations_4.jpg/440px-SADF-Operations_4.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SADF-Operations_4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
South African paratroops on a raid in Angola, 1980s</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>South Africa had a policy of attacking insurgent bases and <a href="/wiki/Safe_house" title="Safe house">safe houses</a> of PLAN and MK in neighbouring countries beginning in the early 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> These attacks were in retaliation for acts of sabotage, urban terrorism, and guerrilla raids by MK, PLAN, and the <a href="/wiki/Azanian_People%27s_Liberation_Army" title="Azanian People's Liberation Army">Azanian People's Liberation Army</a> (APLA). The country also aided organisations in surrounding countries who were actively combating the spread of communism in southern Africa. The results of these policies included:</p>
<ul>
<li>Support for guerrilla groups such as <a href="/wiki/UNITA" title="UNITA">UNITA</a> in Angola and <a href="/wiki/RENAMO" title="RENAMO">RENAMO</a> in Mozambique</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/South_African_Defence_Force" title="South African Defence Force">South African Defence Force</a> (SADF) raids into front-line states (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Raid_on_Gaborone" title="Raid on Gaborone">Raid on Gaborone</a>). Bombing raids were also conducted into neighbouring states. Air and commando raids into Zimbabwe, Zambia, and Botswana occurred the same day, against ANC targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></li>
<li>An assassination attempt on <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mugabe" title="Robert Mugabe">Robert Mugabe</a>, Prime Minister and future President of Zimbabwe, on 18 December 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanlon_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-hanlon-157">[157]</a></sup></li>
<li>A <a href="/wiki/Operation_Savannah_(Angola)" title="Operation Savannah (Angola)">full-scale intervention into Angola</a>: this was partly in support of UNITA, but was also an attempt to strike at PLAN bases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></li>
<li>Bomb attacks in <a href="/wiki/Lesotho" title="Lesotho">Lesotho</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanlon_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-hanlon-157">[157]</a></sup></li>
<li>Kidnapping of refugees and ANC members in <a href="/wiki/Swaziland" title="Swaziland">Swaziland</a> by security services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanlon_157-2"><a href="#cite_note-hanlon-157">[157]</a></sup></li>
<li>An unsuccessful South African organised coup in the <a href="/wiki/Seychelles" title="Seychelles">Seychelles</a> on 25 November 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanlon_157-3"><a href="#cite_note-hanlon-157">[157]</a></sup></li>
<li>Targeting of exiled ANC leaders abroad: Joe Slovo's wife <a href="/wiki/Ruth_First" title="Ruth First">Ruth First</a> was killed by a parcel bomb in Maputo, and "death squads" of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Cooperation_Bureau" title="Civil Cooperation Bureau">Civil Cooperation Bureau</a> and the Directorate of Military Intelligence attempted to carry out assassinations on ANC targets in <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>, Paris,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>, and London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>In 1984, Mozambican president <a href="/wiki/Samora_Machel" title="Samora Machel">Samora Machel</a> signed the <a href="/wiki/Nkomati_Accord" title="Nkomati Accord">Nkomati Accord</a> with South Africa's president <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P.W._Botha" title="P.W. Botha">P.W. Botha</a>, in an attempt to end South African support for the opposition group <a href="/wiki/RENAMO" title="RENAMO">RENAMO</a>. South Africa agreed to cease supporting anti-government forces, while the MK was prohibited from operating in Mozambique. This was a setback for the ANC. Machel hoped the agreement would alliterate<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (February 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mozambique_Civil_War" title="Mozambique Civil War">civil war</a> and allow Mozambique to rebuild its economy. Two years later, President Machel was killed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mozambican_Tupolev_Tu-134_air_disaster" title="Mozambican Tupolev Tu-134 air disaster">an air crash</a> in mountainous terrain in South Africa near the Mozambican border after returning from a meeting in Zambia. South Africa was accused by the Mozambican government and US Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/George_P._Shultz" title="George P. Shultz">George P. Shultz</a> of continuing its aid to RENAMO. The Mozambican government also made an unproven allegation that the accident was caused intentionally by a false <a href="/wiki/Radio_navigation" title="Radio navigation">radio navigation</a> beacon that scrambled the aircraft's navigational system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> These charges were never proven and is still a subject of some controversy, despite the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mozambican_Tupolev_Tu-134_air_disaster#Margo_Commission" title="Mozambican Tupolev Tu-134 air disaster">South African Margo Commission</a> finding that the crash was an accident. A Soviet delegation that did not participate in the investigation issued a minority report implicating South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beginning in 1966, PLAN, armed wing of the <a href="/wiki/SWAPO" title="SWAPO">South-West African People's Organisation</a> (SWAPO), contested South Africa's occupation of South-West Africa (now Namibia). This conflict deepened after <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a> gained its independence in 1975 under the leadership of the leftist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Popular_Movement_for_the_Liberation_of_Angola" title="Popular Movement for the Liberation of Angola">Popular Movement for the Liberation of Angola</a> (MPLA) aided by <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>. South Africa, Zaire and the United States sided with the Angolan rival <a href="/wiki/UNITA" title="UNITA">UNITA</a> party against the MPLA's armed force, FAPLA (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Armed_Forces_for_the_Liberation_of_Angola" title="People's Armed Forces for the Liberation of Angola">People's Armed Forces for the Liberation of Angola</a>). The following struggle turned into one of several late Cold War flashpoints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angolan_civil_war" title="Angolan civil war">Angolan civil war</a> developed into a conventional war with South Africa and UNITA on one side against the <a href="/wiki/MPLA" title="MPLA">MPLA</a> government, the Soviet Union, the Cubans and SWAPO on the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANC_definition_of_apartheid_in_1977_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-ANC_definition_of_apartheid_in_1977-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="State_security">State security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: State security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>During the 1980s the government, led by P.W. Botha, became increasingly preoccupied with security. It set up a powerful <a href="/wiki/State_Security_Council" title="State Security Council">state security apparatus</a> to "protect" the state against an anticipated upsurge in political violence that the reforms were expected to trigger. The 1980s became a period of considerable political unrest, with the government becoming increasingly dominated by Botha's circle of generals and police chiefs (known as securocrats), who managed the various States of Emergencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>Botha's years in power were marked also by numerous military interventions in the states bordering South Africa, as well as an extensive military and political campaign to eliminate SWAPO in Namibia. Within South Africa, meanwhile, vigorous police action and strict enforcement of security legislation resulted in hundreds of arrests and bans, and an effective end to the ANC's sabotage campaign.</p>
<p>The government punished political offenders brutally. 40,000 people were subjected to <a href="/wiki/Flagellation" title="Flagellation">whipping</a> as a form of punishment annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> The vast majority had committed political offences and were lashed ten times for their crime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> If convicted of treason, a person could be hanged, and the government executed numerous political offenders in this way.</p>
<p>As the 1980s progressed, more and more anti-apartheid organisations were formed and affiliated with the UDF. Led by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reverend" title="Reverend">Reverend</a> <a href="/wiki/Allan_Boesak" title="Allan Boesak">Allan Boesak</a> and Albertina Sisulu, the UDF called for the government to abandon its reforms and instead abolish apartheid and eliminate the homelands completely.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_of_emergency">State of emergency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apartheid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: State of emergency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Serious political violence was a prominent feature from 1985 to 1989, as black townships became the focus of the struggle between anti-apartheid organisations and the Botha government. Throughout the 1980s, township people resisted apartheid by acting against the local issues that faced their particular communities.
